RATING: 9
4 Player Game: 9/10. The four player game fixed all of the problems below. The drafting, while it took a longer time, made the game more balanced. The person who won actually beat all of us by  creating a Valhalla engine that was giving him 5 points for each figure that he released from Valhalla (ended up with a 55 point swing). I was going for the quest route and lost by about 10 points. The game is much longer with 4 players (especially if some are new to the game), but it makes the game much more tense, exciting and balanced. 



2-player game: 4/10. It's kind of hard to rate as I only played a two-player game which was a horrible idea. The mechanics and game play were decent, and obviously the miniatures are amazing, but the two player game was horrendous. First round, my wife won a huge battle by playing  a +5  from her hand, and that was it for me. After being decimated in that battle, I could not come back. She was able to freely pillage the board while I had to spend the rest of my turns getting people back on the board.  Since the warriors carry over, she maintained her dominance the rest of the game. Also I had the misfortune of drafting 0 quest cards during the final round--making it literally impossible to win; I just forfeited instead. So, while I can see that there is a good game at the core here, this is one of the worst two player games I have ever played; a game like Inis has a much better battle system for 2.

RATING: 6
[url=https://www.boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/207536/item/4562339]1 play[/url]

The game is not complicated. You draft cards each round, which you can use to customize your clan (they all start the same). These give you new abilities, recruit additional forces, etc. They are associated with one of several gods, each with a particular style of effect (e.g., better combat, more VP, tricky effects). After the draft, everyone takes actions one at a time -- move on the board, attack, etc. At the end of the round, VP is awarded for control of one of the regions (specified at the start of the round, subject to change based on some card effects).

There are some interesting systems at work, but you definitely have to be aware of the card powers and such -- I, at least, would need additional plays! This might be worth another go despite the drafting, although I doubt it will become a favorite of mine.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Only a few games in right now, so the rating is subject to change. The game is pretty to look at, has great concepts and found a way to avoid the issue of the "drôle de guerre", where no player wants to attack because of its weakening effect on one's forces. It's a stretched logic though: I understand the glory of dying in combat, but it becomes a rather poor justification for voluntarily killing tons of units through suicidal VP generation.

My biggest gripe with Blood Rage right now is how chaotic the games have turned out to be. Card effect ranges from "slight advantage" to "game changer" or even "VP maelstrom". When you trigger a conflict (pillage) you just have to hope some wild effect will not turn your well-thought plans into a shockingly bad move, but there is not that much you can do to anticipate. Costs seems off too. There is a "1-cost" card that allows you to get a VP for every unit lost at the end of an age, while other costlier cards have very situational effects and/or lower VP potential. Green cards are a bit curious too. It's usually pretty easy to fill the requirements, having not to win a territory, but only dominate in one of the region's territory. It's hard to tell that a player is even trying to fill a quest requirement by looking at its actions, and the fight for territorial dominance has rarely been tensed and harshly contested.

Sure, you can technically see several cards in play in a given age through the draft; sure, the draft tends to create a "somewhat" equal distribution of strong cards, but I have started to see this mechanism as a warning label: how wild can you go with cards imbalance by simply telling yourself, as a designer, that the draft will just even things out? And the logic has its limits too. In any case, all of this brings the game closer to Ameritrash than I expected when I was reading the rules.

Overall, this turns out more negative that I wanted it to be, and I have yet to play more, but I had high expectations.

--

Was extremely skeptical about this game and was turned off by the hype it had. Played this weekend and... amazing.

The theming and miniatures are spot on of course, but the gameplay is really where it shines. I really liked the drafting in Blood Rage. A lot of tension in choosing which cards to keep and which cards to pass to the other players.

I was also pleasantly surprised that Blood Rage played more like a euro, yet it still scratched that "fight and kill with my units on a board" itch. I liked that there were a lot of strategic decisions in how much you might commit to Yggdrasil or if you might sacrifice units for more glory or whether to focus more on battles or quests and how that affected the drafting phase.

Just altogether a great package. Simple but deep. Random enough with the drafting that it's pretty fair, even for those who have not played before. Love it.

RATING: N/A
WTP 1/4 [AREA CONTROL] 3-4 players; 60-90 minutes; The love child of Midgard and Chaos in the Old World (who was unfortunately born with the defect of card drafting)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Inexplicably popular point-salad euro in Ameritash clothing. Miniatures are deployed to the regions of the game board, which can be "pillaged". If uncontested, the player takes the value of the bonus tile there; if contested, all present players play an attack bonus card, add up their units' strengths and the winner scores glory points from the battle. It's also possible to score glory from special cards, from dead units, from completed quests (typically have the most strength in a specific region at the end of the round), having units in a specific location destroyed at the end of the round and so on. Timing, tension, blah blah blah.

Despite the miniatures -- not Games Workshop calibre but otherwise excellent: better than previous CMoN games and substantially better than "boardgame quality" -- it's very much a euro in style. The key strategic element is the card drafting at the start of each round, and the issues typical of that mechanism are compounded by the limited card pool (many single cards) and strong focus on card synergy. A player's success is to some degree predicated on both random card distribution and on their opponents' willingness to hate-draft.

The rest of the play is highly tactical, with broader strategic concerns emerging chiefly in determining which units to leave on the board for the next round and where. But drafting again makes this difficult; planning for specific quests, for example, is unreliable. As expected from an Eric Lang game, chaotic special card powers abound, so there is certainly no such thing as a sure thing. 

This is another hybrid design that seems to me like the worst of both worlds. From its Ameritrash lineage it gets expensive miniatures and random effects; on the euro side it gets unthematic, abstract point-salad. The theme works only if you try very hard.

Don't get me wrong, the game does work. There are, being charitable, multiple paths to victory (uncharitable: several ingredient ratios to compose a salad), and genuine player differentiation emerges from the card draft. There is significant direct player interaction. It's just not enough.

What I find particularly bizarre are the favourable comparisons to Cthulhu Wars, a game with some similar mechanisms but genuine asymmetry, much more focused gameplay and much more theme. I would choose it in a heartbeat ahead of this one.

If Stefan feld were to design a wargame it might look like this.

RATING: 7
Really clean game design.

In my first game I spanked everyone else at the table.  I had a really good first age and managed to pillage the central area.  Then in the second age I got wolfman, which combined with some Tyr's cards basically let me "halifax hammer" my way to victory.

I had the ability to keep my cards after combat.  I had the Wolfman who meant that each fight was only with monsters (so I couldn't be beaten by sheer numbers) and my Tyr's battle cards combined with my Tyr's clan upgrade and the battle card that let me play it for 3 strength after everyone had played their battle cards meant that I had a guaranteed 11 strength in every fight and my opponents could only use monsters in battle, meaning that they had to come up with at least 7 damage to beat me (which didn't happen).

I was able to get an early lead and in this game I never lost the fight for the centre and by the end I was almost apologising I had so badly snowballed the game.

I also had Fenrir who I used on the final round to grab 20 points.  I had to work for it though and it felt great when it came off.

Honestly this game was spoiled by the Wolfman.

RATING: 7
Solid game, but for all the things it has going for it (upgradeable race, nice miniatures, multiple ways to score points) it remains a king-of-the-hill style type of game with two main gripes:
- if you have a newbie on the table the player sitting clockwise from him is going to win the game as he basically has two piles to draft from
- sometimes you just don't get the cards you are aiming for but you realise it too late, being screwed before you even play that round. Of course, this is your own fault, but is it fun?

Won't mind playing this game, but much prefer Chaos in the Old World to which this game is often compared. I'm also surprised by the comparison as it feels more of a Nexus Ops with drafting. That being said, we have one non KS-copy in our gaming group, it is a solid game to play but quickly needs another expansion to really stay fresh. Or a KS copy which will cost an arm and a leg and then some. Not going that way.

RATING: 9

RATING: 7
Pretty good game except for a couple features:  a player can fill a territory completely and own it round after round, and there's absolutely nothing you can do about it.  If they draft quests for it (which can just be lucky to get one from the first draw and otherwise requires opponents to hate-draft and weaken themselves as a result - i.e. the player that takes the quest and doesn't need it helps all of the other players to his own detriment) and have the late game Ragnarok zone, it can really be frustrating, again, because there's just nothing you can do about it, literally.

RATING: 7
tl;dr: an overproduced, more aggressive [thing=23985][/thing]

I always thought [thing=23985][/thing] was an underappreciated design. It is a very clean, streamlined area majority game. Thanks to this new implementation, it gets more attention. Too bad, in my opinion, they made the design less clean and made it more of a conflict game and less of an area majority game.
It is still a very good design, but it does move away from the aspects I respected the most about it.
The minis seem to be there mostly to sell more copies. They are very nice, but I would have preferred wooden pieces. Also, the minis sometimes make it hard to tell what figure you're looking at.
I haven't played Midgard in years, but playing this, I want to try it again.
As for this version: I wouldn't mind playing it again, but there's no way I'm paying for a copy of this version.

RATING: 7
Excellent miniatures and terrific art are the frosting on this game, which is a battle for glory. The unpredictable card element of a fantasy board game like Talisman is made more interesting here with a more immediate focus, multiple characters per player (leaders, warriors, sea ships, and monsters), more of a reliance upon strategy, and a board that evolves throughout the three different phases of play. Add to this the GREAT mechanic of self-sacrifice possibly working in a player's favor--and thus, a player's goals shifting mid-game or being hidden from others at first--and the resultant game has a lot of possibilities. This is not an analysis paralysis monster like Mage Knight (my favorite solitaire game alongside Thunderbolt Apache Leader and Perdition's Mouth), but there are often tough choices and gambles to be made.
A couple things are counterintuitive--I'd rather that warriors could not "March" to non-adjacent lands and the term "Invade" is not well chosen for landing new pieces on the board ("Invade" sounds like an action that would occur after a "March")--but these are minor quibbles.
Overall Blood Rage is a really enjoyable game that has some of the immersive qualities of far heavier games while being fast moving, unpredictable, and a very good balance of luck and strategy. (One aside: In every instance that I can recall, the re-pillaging card massively warped the game in the card user's favor. I won't say that this card fully breaks the game--I can think of some theoretical ways around it--but it affects things more than any other card I've seen, by far, and I wish it weren't in mix.)
My favorite board game is Cave Evil while my girlfriend adores Pandemic, and Blood Rage is game that we both have a great time playing together. It is immediate, goal-driven fun and an engaging fantasy with lots of choices and great visuals.

RATING: 8.5
It is a fairly straightforward game. Unlike many of my favorite games, I never find myself reflecting on a game of Blood Rage that I just played wondering "what if I had done that?" or "what if I try such-and-such instead next time?"... I just don't think there is enough meat to the decisions in order to provide the kind of experience that would warrant this kind of reflection afterwards. 

Nonetheless, I love this game! It is a clean design that makes for a smooth playing experience with just enough tension and surprises. What this game lacks in strategic depth it more than makes up for in pure excitement. It is just so damn fun! After the first few games you know exactly what combos there are and the various strategies that exist, but it is still so fun to see how it plays out. I really don't know how to describe it.

NOTE: Everybody seems to think this game is all about the draft. I actually think the draft at the beginning of the round isn't that important. The key to the game lies more with timing. Knowing exactly WHEN to play a card seems more important to me than actually getting some of the supposedly overpowered combos.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Blood Rage has gotten a lot of hype, and usually that makes me less likely to enjoy a game.  But, I was pleasantly surprised by it.  Blood Rage is really hard to classify.  It has area control and awesome miniatures, but also has action point allowance and drafting.  It's kind of a dudes on the map game, but there is so much more to it than just controlling territory.  I often dislike multiplayer area control games because of the politics/kingmaking, but Blood Rage avoids those pitfalls.

I went for a strategy that rewards you for losing battles, and I actually pulled out a solid victory.  I got points for losing battles, points for getting my guys killed, and I was able to place more guys whenever I lost a battle.  The fact that this strategy even exists boosted by rating by a full point.  Everything about the game was enjoyable and I didn't have any complaints.  I could see this rating rising if I get more plays.

UPDATE: My second play of Blood Rage was underwhelming, perhaps because it was only with 3 players.  Once again the strategy of losing battle dominated the game, although this time someone else used it.  There are a lot of great mechanisms put together in this game, but I'm not sure the sum of the mechanisms is something I enjoy.  I'd play it again, but never ask for it.

RATING: 8.5
I think Blood Rage stands atop the mountain of bodies in a very competitive sub-genre of 90-120 minute dudes on a map bloodbaths.  This crowded field includes other great titles in recent years such as Kemet, Cyclades, and Cthulhu Wars (which definitely comes in fourth on this list).

What makes Blood Rage reign supreme is two-fold: 1) the card draw before each of the three rounds allows the players to develop and support flexible strategies, and 2) the tight geography and immediacy of conflict leads to plenty of bloodshed.  

The minis are fantastic, too, but all the games listed above have at least equal bragging rights in that department.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This game is a lighter version of Chaos in the old world withthe  most distinct difference being that there are no variable player powers (and of course the altered combat resolution). Which tickles a diferent player styles' fancy! 
In Citow your playstyle is prescripted according to the demon god you get and the associated deck of cards. In Blood Rage you can alter the playstyle to your liking according to the cards you pick in the draft. 
For instance, if you randomly assign roles in Citow and you draw Khorne you have to stick with a very aggressive playstyle to benefit from Khornes power. But there are people who just don't like to play agressive. They love to build up and defend. Others happen to hate this and love direct confrontation. Either way Citow maybe forces you to play in a way you don't enjoy. As said before in Blood Rage you can draft cards according to your "liking" and even change plans in between when you see that a specific route didn't work out.The advantage being that several routes can lead to succes, with the felt disadvantage being that maybe too many routes can lead to success and that in the end your plans - when working out - don't feel rewarding. Nevertheles I think this is a very good game

RATING: 8.5
Early comments.  This game is a bit of a rush--and that can be either good or bad.  The presentation is exceptional--though quite a bit behind what I consider to be the vanguard of presentation in Scythe.  Art/bits are certainly eye catching, though there is a bit of trade off in the small cards and cardstock sideboards and player mats.

But:  gameplay is swift and intense.  Love the myriad of ways to play, but that can also lead to unforeseen surprises (I'm looking at you, Heimdall and Loki), and to well laid plans being laid to waste.  I love it, but I can also see why someone with different play styles or expectations would be lead to dislike the game, or even hate it.

RATING: 10
I have to join in on the unanimous praise for this game. It is excellent and I can't really see any negatives for now. The minis are fantastic, the gameplay is streamlined and simple to learn, a lot of cards feel overpowered but in a fun way. Awesome.

Still not sure where I'll land on Blood Rage versus Chaos in the Old World. CitOW is a solid 10 for me, but Blood Rage cleans up some of the fiddlier parts by just removing them completely (dominance, dial advancements, corruption, event tokens).

This game also has the advantage of playing with different player counts. It seems to be just as good with 3-5 players (skip it with two players). CitOW is really 4 players only, and I wouldn't even recommend the fifth player expansion for that game.

Great game, could possibly be a 10 after more plays.

Update: It's a 10. There's tons of tension in every move and so far it seems like different strategies can win.

I will say that the game definitely seems to be best with four players. Mechanically everything is fine with three players, but with four players you have more people going for different strategies and keeping each other in check.

So far I think that this game and CitOW offer different enough experiences to warrant owning both. Although a lot of the gameplay elements are the same, Blood Rage offers a "choose your own strategy" feeling, where CitOW gives you a strategy from the beginning based on which god you choose.

[b]Player Count Verdict: 3-4[/b]
Just not good with two players. You need players taking different strategies and keeping each other in check for the game to work, so the game is best with four, and good with three. With five it can be a bit long and harder to plan.

RATING: 9
I really enjoy Blood Rage. Although it can have big changes in points, this adds to my enjoyment of the game as it can allow a trailing player to stay in the game through clever play using an alternative path to victory such as getting all of your dudes killed and scoring that way. Definitely better at higher player counts (4, 5) as I find in a 3 player game it is more difficult to reign in the first age leader. As with many "plastic men on cardboard" this is group dependent. Can be cleverly strategic but benefits from a group willing to interact with table talk and curtailing the leader. 
For new players it takes some time to learn the card combos when drafting. I usually point out several of the paths to victory, especially that a player can win by both successfully pillaging and by getting their figures killed and sent to Valhalla. 
Definitely recommend, have played this 15-20 times at all player counts.

RATING: 10
This game has pushed previous favourites down, or even completely off, my shelf. Game of Thrones and Kemet used to see a lot more plays before I got my hands on Blood Rage. I'd definitely say it has replaced Game of Thrones for me, while I still see chances to play Kemet. 

This game just nails everything for me. From the viking theme, the amazing minis (that have found their way into my D&D campaigns) to the mix of Euro (get points) and American-style (fight) games. The drafting definitely pushes this game up; I love the mechanic as it allows for on-the-fly strategizing and gives you a chance to mess with your opponents.

Own 5th Player, Gods & Mystics expansions.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I had heard this game was the hotness, but it didn't really capture my interest. It's a direct conflict game and it's all about the miniatures, two things that don't really sell a game for me.

My play was colored by the fact that the rules explanation was all over the place. Some things were explained partially, then we'd switch to a new topic and only later would we finish the first part. Rules kept appearing with a "Oh, I forgot... one more thing". And another thing that colored the play for me was that the player explaining the game had played before and had memorized the cards, while the other two players were new.

The game has some interesting bits. It's essentially and area control-ish type of thing, where there's competition to be able to trigger fights in areas to get bonuses, which usually increase you ability to have more units on the board, get Vp's from fights, or take more actions. There's a draft before each of the three rounds, which factors into your ability to do things.

My main complaints about the game was that as a new player, you're at a disadvantage not knowing the cards, or how the game really works. And some of the cards seem really overpowered. There was one in particular that allowed a player to place a free unit whenever he brought a unit in that really seemed wonky.

It was interesting, but seemed to have some flaws, and wasn't really my thing.

RATING: 6
It's not painful to play, so that's great.

A number of issues though. It seems like a game about conquering the map, but players seem reluctant to engage in battles they could lose; battles seem to mostly happen because as a defender you can't retreat units when somebody picks a fight.

The engine building is simple and doesn't seem to offer a lot of strategies. It also seems to create runaway leaders, partly because nobody sees it as a good idea to sacrifice their own plans just to make trouble for the leader.

The sense that the map matters is weakened by the unit movement action, which allows teleporting anywhere. Though, when someone starts an attack somewhere, other players have the option of moving in units from adjacent regions only.

RATING: 8.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
So chances are if you're reading reviews about Blood Rage you kind of already know you want it. Admit it. Deep down the decision has already been made.

What you get in the box is the good kind of ridiculous. The minis by CMON are some of the best I've ever seen. Ever. The monsters are INCREDIBLE. It's so hard to pass up the chance to claim them in the game because few things feel better than clicking one of these beasties into your clan's ring and smashing it down onto the table. I wish I was talented enough to paint them in a way that would do them justice because hot. Damn. 

I really quite like Blood Rage's take on area control too, because everything is so fast and crazy and disposable. There isn't any turtling or slow play, because dagnabbit you're vikings and vikings wanna smash things and die gloriously. And with the scoring mechanics focusing on this idea, rounds can fly by blazingly fast. It is very rare for you to feel too upset about losing a battle due to the (sometimes broken) battle cards because:
a) every card and upgrade in this game feels equally, gloriously broken and
b) your units come back from Valhalla before you can say Yggdrassil. And they will probably score you points for doing so.

I've found that in most games under 4 players somebody unfortunately is wailed-on and put at *too* much of a disadvantage to really come back and challenge for the win. But Blood Rage is so fast and fun that this usually doesn't mar the experience too much.

It has been a tough call for me to choose between this and Inis as my favourite gorgeous-area-control-and-card-drafting game, but after many plays and much deliberation I am leaning more towards Inis as being the better of the two. 

But like I said, you know you already want this game. Come on.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Wow, is this a tough game to rate. I really like Chaos In The Old World (without the expansion), so I expected to LOVE this. After all, the gameplay is largely similar to CITOW, but with drafting (a mechanic I love), scalability (CITOW should only be played with 4) and flexibility (no need adhering to a fixed strategy depending on the god you play). My first game of it was great, but also a little disappointing. Casual gamers found it to be harder than CITOW, because they found it hard to assess the relative worth of the cards going round. On top of that, the game feels more luck dependent, as some of the cards seem really strong and allow for some incredible combos. And since there are only three drafting rounds, it can be really hard to catch up with the leader after the first Valhalla fase. Finally, I personally found that the third round was not as interesting as the first two, as there was less to do with the shrinking board - cards get more powerful, but also more expensive. However, I still like the game and think it looks gorgeous - so I am curious to find out how my next game will go.

RATING: 9
One of my all-time favorite games. The card drafting phase provides a great deal of tension as you try to balance the cost of allowing another great card slip into the hands of your opponents. The combat phase itself allows for a lot of room for decision making, and every turn feels just as impactful as the other. The way conflict is handled also allows for mind games, and Blood Rage makes it so that even losing can have its perks. That being said, certain aspects of Blood Rage can be very swingy, and a few cards feel significantly better than others leading to a small imbalance that nullifies certain strategies. That being said, I love Blood Rage, and I look forward to playing many more games in the future.

RATING: 8
Initial rating, may rise or fall some with further plays.  Time will tell if the game feels played out at some point.  Definitely a game that hits it home with me.  I miss the feeling of area control and tech building some... This game is almost purely attacking, but there are a TON of tactics and strategy to your actions.

My big complaint is how swingy the game is, you really  never have any idea who is winning as someone can come out of nowhere with 60-80 points on any age.  It's also perhaps a bit too crazy? Not as crazy as Cosmic Encounter, you can learn what the power moves are, but it can be really hard to guess what your opponent is up to.  The game can be really punishing if you make one bad move so it's almost stressful in that way to the point where it's almost stressful to play.  You might have a set of actions in your hand, a plan in your head, suddenly places are getting ransacked faster than you expected, there's no time to do everything you wanted to do! Tough decisions is the name of this game, to a painful degree.

Overall very enjoyable still... the combo's just don't quite come together as exciting for me as in other games.

RATING: 5
An impressive feat of game design, with neat minis, but I simply didn't find it very compelling.  All-or-nothing combat resolution systems like this one usually lead to pile-on-the-leader slugfests that devolve into horrendously long drags, but this game neatly avoids the problem by giving a lot of incentive to move around and cards that progressively get better as the game moves along.  It's a tough problem to solve, and the fact that this game has done so is admirable.  But then I'm left pretty cold by the whole experience.  The theme here is supposedly viking, but the execution of the thematic flourishes feels like it could be set in any of a number of other generic universes.  Euros have gotten flak for Egypt=Italy=Netherlands=... themes with no real difference, but it's getting to the point where Ameritrash is falling into the same traps, but instead with Viking=Fantasy=Warhammer=... themes.  I'm not going to insist on something else if this comes out, but I'm not super excited to play it either.

RATING: 9
As we all know from the Nordic mythology and sagas, the vikings invented the drafting mechanism now widely used in many board and card games as the way to divide the spoils from their raids. So very appropriately, card drafting is the core mechanism which drives the epic, build your own special powers engine dudes on a map game that is Blood Rage.

Over three ages players have to make agonising decisions to choose between the many cool special powers and quests to put together an efficient combat engine. The viking clans and their house-trained giant monsters then slug it out for glory in the provinces around Yggdrasil while the whole world is falling apart in Ragnarok. "We need a bigger Valhalla". The casualties pile up high in Valhalla, often with a sneaky happy grin on their players' faces as they wanted them to be slaughtered in battle for the glory of being resurrected from Valhalla at the end of each age.

Very good, in your face confrontational game with lots of hard choices to be made throughout and with great miniatures (bigatures?/maxiatures?). Highly recommended Eurotrash game. Definitely a keeper to sit proudly next to Kemet and Cyclades.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
'Blood Rage' is an excellent game, themed on the Norse gods' final battle with the giants of Jontheim and the God of Mischief; Loki. Each player takes on the role of one of up to four clans who are vying to improve their prestige, through earned glory (victory points), by conquest and strategy. The clans start with a leader and four warriors, and can utilise these in a number of ways using rage as the resource to do so, which along with glory and horns, are the three abilities that need to be increased to achieve more glory. There are three turns, consisting of card selection, actions including battle and pillaging, and finally Ragnarok itself. Players take one of eight cards at the beginning of each turn, selecting one and then passing the balance on to the player to their left, until the final two for each player are placed in the discard deck. Any unused cards, during the turn are also discarded, bar one, before the next selection round apart from turn three of course at the end of which Ragnarok is well and truly here! The cards contain things such as battle bonuses from allies, clan upgrades, like improved combat effectiveness, monsters, quests and ship upgrades. Most of these have rage point costs, that must be paid to acquire, being then placed in the clan player board (homeland). There are several realms surrounding Yggdrasil, and one of these is destroyed by placement of a Ragnarok counter, and three more of these will be destroyed in each of the three turns, in accordance with the predetermined draw. This needs to be watched, as players may not necessarily want their forces to be destroyed in the cataclysm!, although certain cards actually encourage this as bonus glory can be earned for this and/or retrieving warriors from Valhalla right at the end of each turn. When cards are utilised subsequently, the rage cost must be paid, and bear in mind (as I failed to do on one occasion), that quests must all be placed before the clan rage pool is exhausted. There are no limits, apparently, to the number of quests that may be placed, although having these does limit options in other areas such as clan upgrades! Gaining higher levels of rage, glory and horns yields glory at the end of the game. I should comment that raising glory increases the glory earned in each battle that is fought and horns increase the number of warriors (there is a maximum of eight) and/or your one leader and/or your one ship that can be placed. in the game we played, Endof Supercycle won following the strategy of sending and retrieving warriors from the halls of Valhalla whereas Rod Chapman collected bonus glory for ending the game with all of his pieces on the table. Both of these were generated by specific clan upgrades which can be played to any clan so long as the player has drawn the card in the first instance. There were no KRO's and the game was well explained by Jim Boardgames. I would make only two observations and that would be that when a pillage attempt is made, each player in turn sequence declares if one of their clan (only those in adjacent realms) wishes to invade. (The number of invading pieces is limited by the number of available unoccupied spaces). And secondly, when any upgrade card is played, each player confirms what it is once, as it is difficult and delays the game if players have to lean over and inspect cards each time they are about to take an action. Should they then forget later, tough cheddar! On further reflection, I wasn't sure why there was a limitation on potential realm spaces as there are none for Yggdrassil itself and each clan is still limited by their overall clan size as mentioned before. I wasn't clear why ships could not move after placement and would be looking for an explanation from the game designer although the expansion(s) may change this arrangement perhaps. Nevertheless, it didn't detract from my enjoyment, even though I gained least glory, and was last. That's the difference between a good and poor game in my view.

RATING: 5
Played a few times. What I like: there is good decision angst, both in the card drafting and action phases. What I don't like: there's just too many vp opportunities flying around. I was hoping there'd be more interaction with players fighting over scraps; instead, I've found the game too self-involved as players are more attentive to the card combos in their hands than each other. There's just too many cards of the "score x points for having y" variety, e.g. in my last game I drafted a card that gave me 12 VP if my boat was destroyed. Getting the boat destroyed was a trivial, uninteresting task that got me beaucoup points without any prior planning or overarching strategy. That's how much of the game starts to feel: why bother guessing which scoring card the other guy has and trying to stop it when you've got your own scoring card to fulfill.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A prettier, more exciting version of Midgard, Eric Lang's 2007 Z-man release. It plays pretty much the same, except that there is a bigger focus on combat and the entire game has been reconfigured to accentuate that. It's hard not to love the new version, as the miniatures are tremendously well done and the whole production pops, especially when compared to the quaint wooden pawns and one dimensional decision making of the original.

Still, while I think it is more engaging than the original, I'm not sure it has legs. The decks from which you draft cards are tiny in number, which means you see the exact same cards almost every game. That may be a positive for game balance, but it sure seems like it will get repetitive after a few plays. There is also a degenerate tactic (at least it feels that way) of shoveling guys into the doomed Raganarok spaces, just to convert them into almost instant points. While marching off to Ragnarok is thematically appropriate, it feels cheap to intentionally avoid battles in order to gain better position for a mass suicide.

I can't in any way say this is a bad game, but I will say there are too many counter-thematic "gamey" moves for me to ever consider it a good one. Maybe if I was a painter, my opinion could be swayed a bit. I have to say the miniatures are fantastic. I suppose the gameplay is good enough, when it's all said and done.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
In many ways, Blood Rage feels like a spiritual successor to Chaos in the Old World. This shouldn't really be that surprising when you realize that Eric Lang designed both games.

In that regard, Blood Rage stands out above CitOW for me because of some of the new elements that it adds. Players now draft hands of cards at the start of each round. Combat is driven by a Cosmic Encounter-like card system instead of dice. Players will have a variety of methods for gaining victory points depending on the cards they drafted, which will skew the way they deploy their troops to the board.

Blood Rage also keeps several of the interesting facets of CitOW: alternate routes to victory, upgrading your army, area control and keeping your opponents in check.

Blood Rage is also more flexible in player count than CitOW. 4 is probably still best but the game still works fine with 3 and even pretty well with 2 players.

Blood Rage does what a good spiritual sequel should and being built on a game that is solid to begin with only makes it great.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I had tried to play Blood Rage once before, but called it because of some botched rules after the first age. So, I was pretty excited when a friend of mine was coming in from out of town to play some games, so another friend of mine came over and I setup Blood Rage.

I’d love to give a play-by-play, but my memory is terrible, however I’ll try to give a general gist of how I played. I managed to draft the clan upgrade that allows you to play a warrior for free whenever you place another figure in a region. Later, I got the warrior upgrade that makes a pair of them worth 3 strength, so I had that synergy. In addition, I also had the clan upgrade that gave me points for figures released from Valhalla, so dying was no longer a big concern. I combined that with the quest to have a certain number of figures in Valhalla to pull out the win.

I love games with asymmetry, whether that’s built-in from the start with games like Chaos in the Old World or whether it’s earned over the course of the game like in Kemet or Blood Rage. The added wrinkle of drafting those differences and trying to create interesting combinations was a lot of fun, and I’m surprised no one had tried it before.

I’m happy I backed this one on Kickstarter and I look forward to playing it a lot more in the future.

Highly recommended if you enjoy confrontational games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I like this game a lot. It is a minis game, but the mechanisms and play are very well defined. There are three ages of the game with several phases each. First there is a drafting round. Next, there is an action phase when plays can upgrade characters, place characters onto the board, establish goals, and pillage provinces. Finally, goals are evaluated, another province is destroyed, and any destroyed figures are released from Valhalla. The game plays well and the minis look really cool. The only thing is now I need to learn to paint minis.

RATING: 9
Review Date: May 2017

Disclaimers for context:I play with the retail available expansions and without. I do not own the kickstarter content

Positives: The models, some of the best out there and once painted make the game a joy to watch/play. The variety of playing styles, it encourages participation whether you win or lose! It is easy to learn but has depth to master. It can vary tremendously using the Gods expansion. 

Negatives:  Experienced players will run over new players until they learn the combos of cards and strategies. Runaway victories are possible. Sometimes forced to hate draft to stop opponents dominating. Some criticize the female models gratuitous clothing 

Conclusion: Excellent card drafting area control game with a real well developed theme and components

Score 9 if you like the battle cry on the way to Valhalla!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I think Blood Rage deserves to be as popular as it is.  There is plenty of strategic depth to be had and lots of fun to be extracted through experimenting with and adapting different strategies.  Wonderful moments of tension can culminate in dramatic surprises in the epic battles which tend to unfold in the Yggdrasil province.  People that suck at strategic combat can even pull out decisive victories by losing on purpose, thanks to the Loki card realm.  As is common knowledge, the production values are top notch, and the theme is fun, well represented, and synergizes well with the gameplay.  

That all being said, the game definitely has some flaws.  The drafting mechanic has little strategy to it beyond "grab the cards that fit MY strategy, if possible, faster than anyone else does," with the only curve ball being "this card doesn't really fit my strategy but I feel I have to take it so that the next player doesn't get to capitalize on it."  Players can also be steamrolled if they fall significantly behind on their "Rage" stat and simply don't have the economy to take as many actions as other players later in the game.  

Despite its flaws, Blood Rage deserves a place in history as one of the best "dudes on a map" games, and a place on your shelf as a game which will most likely be enjoyable for all players that enter your home, new and veteran alike.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Best game of 2015, what a blast to play.  I love how the map keeps shrinking and the models look great.  This game sets the bar high for other games in its genre.  I think Eric Lang has really out done himself here folks, what a fantastic game to play!

I own the Kickstarter version, the extra models are not needed but they are very nice to have.  This game will see a lot of play with my group :)

RATING: 5
It's OK in a 7 wonders way. Cards get passed around and you try to get the best pairings of strategically complimentary abilities that others aren't competing in. Then you play them to move plastic around the map (with combat a bit like the game of thrones boardgame) deciding which leading opponents you want to butt heads with. You also get some fun tech tracks to raise. It plays lightning fast. Like most of these card passing games you fight more against what randomness gives you than each other making this a safe game for those who are combat averse, but also failing to have as great a punch if its direct competition you are looking for.

I find other games where strategies you select to pursue are not dependent on random factors but player design vastly superior. In the end what you have here is a single hand of poker that takes two hours to play.

RATING: 9
Ill break my commentary down based on the player counts Ive had:

2 player: Had 2 games at two players, didnt mind it at two but both plays had a clear runaway winner, but both games at this count were learning games one for me and the other for someone I introduced the game to. Still feels pretty competitive at 2 and games are usually about a hour or so in length decent at this maybe a few more players would be better..

5 Player:  Absolute carnage and chaos, especially in the later ages, the first age saw a lot of the players take early pillages to get the stat pumps and try to avoid incursions, most of the first age was a ramp up to the last two where it was almost kemet like with players decimating each other for vps and pillage blocks. It is Much harder to pillage in the later ages of the game if you dont have a significant force, and getting multiple pillages is doable but maybe more of a pipe dream. The game took a very long time though at this player count so I feel maybe one less player would be ideal, but I wont turn down a game with 5 in the future.

RATING: 3
A friend brought Blood Rage over for our regular games night and it was a very strange gaming experience. I lost a battle early in the 1st Age, which didn't seem like a problem until I realised that that one battle had basically knocked me out of the game. Don't get me wrong, I tried my best to get back into it, but all my efforts were futile and served only to ensure that my last place points tally wasn't too far behind the other players. My game was in marked contrast to the player that won, who was racking up huge scores for fun, despite not having done a great deal more than anyone else to earn the points. I'm not sure what this game is supposed to be, or who it's supposed to be aimed at. The gameplay seems quite limited and it doesn't make a great deal of sense thematically: the chrome really only serving to hide what is, at heart, a pretty basic area control game with a weak combat mechanic layered on top. After playing I checked on BGG and was quite surprised to see that it's ranked 23. I thought it was fairly poor and with a bit of tweaking could have climbed up to 'okay', but in no way could it be described as the 23rd best game in existence. It's certainly not in my top 23 and probably wouldn't make it into my top 23 games with blood in the title.

RATING: 5
I've only logged one play but it wasn't a very good experience.  I respect the attempt: the figures are awesome, the ability to recruit monsters and upgrade your warriors is great, and the Norse mythology theme is terrific.  

But Blood Rage does little to solve the problems inherent to all targeted-attack games: Player A expends resources effing up Player B, and Players C and D are the ultimate winners of the exchange.  That's just not fun for me no matter which of those four players I am.  Plus it incentivizes politicking and whining.

RATING: 4
Finally got to play this. Must say I'm seriously underwhelmed. Didn't do too poorly -- came in 2nd I think. But the winner ran away with it courtesy of Dark Elf + Tyr's Challenge and racking up 60pts on the Rage/Axes/Horns stat via Ygdrassil.

I found the game rather boring. I'll give it another go just to ensure it wasn't just the fact it was the first game. But having played it I can't see why anyone would play this more than 4 or 5 times. There's just not much "game" there. Once you know the powerful (perhaps broken?) combinations of cards, then that person will run away with it. Where's the joy in that? One might argue that once everyone is equal in terms of knowledge everyone will have sufficiently "broken" combos that it balances out, but luck of the draw typically doesn't work that way. It's more likely one of the players will get a broken set resulting in too much power and that person wins. Maybe now and again two would get similarly equally powerful and broken sets, but that would be rare. 

And I fail to understand what the justification is in being able to move everything except your boat. It makes zero sense. The boat goes to a fjord and is stuck there. Stupid. 

I personally think the only reason this game has the popularity it has is the minis. And minis do nothing for me, so I concentrate on the game. And there's not much game here.

Quick and fun to play. Really nice realisation of the endless battles and pride of ending your life on the battlefield to reach the Valhalla. Needs some thinkking ahead, but once you're into it, seems fairly obvious. Simple rules, yet deep strategy as they say it.

RATING: 10
Card drafting plus vikings and monsters fighting during Ragnarok. What's not to love. This is easily one of my favorite games. There's a variety of winning strategies and ways to customize your faction, providing for loads of replayability.

RATING: 8.5
So few games, it seems, can provide both a thematic, engaging experience, *and* lots of tight, balanced mechanisms. Blood Rage does it.

The production of the game is also fantastic, with solid artwork and amazing plastic miniatures. 

I can see this one appealing to lots of folks. Check it out.
